Generated Hypothesis Portfolio

H1 primary score 0.485; inference inferred
In multi-layer language models, the effective number of features that can be linearly decoded from the final layer is greater than the bound for a single layer, because earlier layers can perform non-linear transformations that reduce feature sparsity or dimensionality before the final linear decoding layer.
H2 incremental score 0.34; inference inferred
In practice, language models do not satisfy the linear decoding assumption for all features; instead, they rely on non-linear decoding for many features, which allows them to store exponentially many features per neuron as in the classical compressed sensing bound, consistent with the superposition hypothesis.
Both selected hypotheses are shown on the front page; treat new ideas as audit targets, not proven facts.

1. Run Summary

This section is the executive checksum for the run. It records the run ID, verifies the most important ordering constraint, and defines the report's audit colors. The key process guarantee is that literature search must occur before hypothesis generation, so the generated idea is grounded in retrieved papers rather than produced from the input PDF alone. Read GREEN/YELLOW/RED as claim-audit labels, not as paper-quality labels.

Run ID: e961e00c-773e-4744-9a4e-9286b5d145fc

Literature search happened before hypothesis generation: YES.

GREEN labels require verified references, bibliographic score >= 0.75, direct support, evidence snippets, provenance, and a written reason. The score column in the verification table is bibliographic identity confidence, not support strength. YELLOW means uncertain, partial, or a novel claim that still needs experiment; RED means not found, unsupported, contradicted, or clearly wrong.

Direct evidenceReference identity is verified, evidence explicitly addresses the claim, and the reason explains the support relation.
Indirect evidenceEvidence is relevant background or motivation, but it does not directly prove the full claim.
Background / absentEvidence is too weak, abstract-only, missing, or only useful as context.
Contradictory evidenceEvidence conflicts with the claim or contains explicit contradiction cues.

Four Error Categories

Unsupported ClaimThe conclusion is not supported by any available input material.
OverclaimThe material supports a weaker conclusion, but the generated claim states a stronger one.
Mis-citationThe cited material is topic-related but does not support the specific claim.
ContradictionThe generated claim conflicts with the cited material.

2. Input Paper Profiles

This section shows the structured profile extracted from each submitted PDF before any external retrieval or hypothesis generation. A profile contains the detected title, authors, abstract, and high-level source claims. These fields are produced by deterministic PDF parsing and profiling heuristics, with optional LLM assistance only when enabled. They are not treated as final truth: if the title, abstract, author list, or key claims are wrong, later search queries, relatedness filtering, literature-map items, and citation audits should be interpreted cautiously.

Clean profileTitle, authors, abstract, and key claims match the PDF; downstream retrieval is likely aligned.
Partial profileSome fields are missing or noisy; search may still work, but map and hypothesis quality should be reviewed manually.
Bad profileTitle/abstract/key claims are visibly wrong; later sections may be answering the wrong paper and should be treated as unreliable.

How Many Features Can a Language Model Store Under the Linear Representation Hypothesis?

Authors: Nikhil Garg, Jon Kleinberg, Kenny Peng

Abstract: We introduce a mathematical framework for the linear representation hypothesis (LRH), which asserts that intermediate layers of language models store features linearly. We separate the hypothesis into two claims: linear representation (features are linearly embedded in neuron activations) and linear accessibility (features can be linearly decoded). We then ask: How many neurons d suffice to both linearly represent and linearly access m features? Classical results in compressed sensing imply that for k-sparse inputs, d = O(k log(m/k)) suffices if we allow non-linear decoding algorithms (Candes and Tao, 2006; Cand`es et al., 2006; Donoho, 2006). However, the additional requirement of linear decoding takes the problem out of the classical compressed sensing, into linear compressed sensing. Our main theoretical result establishes nearly-matching upper and lower bounds for linear compressed sensing. We prove that d = Ωϵ( k2 log k log(m/k)) is required while d = Oϵ(k2 log m) suffices. The lower bound establishes a quantitative gap between classical and linear com- pressed setting, illustrating how linear accessibility is a meaningfully stronger hypothesis than linear representation alone. The upper bound confirms that neurons can store an expo- nential number of features under the LRH, giving theoretical evidence for the “superposition hypothesis” (Elhage et al., 2022). The upper bound proof uses standard random constructions of matrices with approx- imately orthogonal columns. The lower bound proof uses rank bounds for near-identity matrices (Alon, 2003) together with Tur´an’s theorem (bounding the number of edges in clique-free graphs). We also show how our results do and do not constrain the geometry of feature representations and extend our results to allow decoders with an activation function and bias.

Key claims:

  • We introduce a mathematical framework for the linear representation hypothesis (LRH), which asserts that intermediate layers of language models store features linearly.
  • The lower bound establishes a quantitative gap between classical and linear compressed setting, illustrating how linear accessibility is a meaningfully stronger hypothesis than linear representation alone.
  • The upper bound proof uses standard random constructions of matrices with approximately orthogonal columns.
  • The lower bound proof uses rank bounds for near-identity matrices (Alon, 2003) together with Tur´an’s theorem (bounding the number of edges in clique-free graphs).

3. Paper Index Legend

This section defines the shorthand paper labels used throughout the report. Labels such as P1, P2, ... refer to retrieved or connected papers after deduplication and relatedness filtering; the internal JSON index remains zero-based, so P1 corresponds to JSON index 0. The relation field explains why a paper entered the candidate pool, for example input seed, cited by the input paper, citing the input paper, semantic expansion, or topical search. The relatedness score is the gate score used before map and hypothesis generation; it is not a citation-support score.

Distance 0 / input seedThe submitted paper itself; useful as context but not enough to prove a new idea.
Distance 1-2Cited-by or citing-neighborhood papers; these are stronger candidates for connected literature.
Semantic expansionTitle/abstract-near papers added when citation graph is sparse; useful but weaker than citation-chain evidence.
Low relatednessRejected or marginal papers should not support hypotheses, even if they are real papers.

4. Literature Map Summary

This section turns the connected paper pool into a literature map. A thread is a cluster of papers that share a method, problem setting, or conceptual role. A gap is a missing test, boundary condition, mechanism, or application regime suggested by the connected evidence. A tension records an unresolved contrast, such as two papers making claims that are hard to compare directly or using incompatible assumptions. A cross-domain connection is a possible transfer of an idea from one setting to another. These items are generated from paper titles, abstracts, retrieved snippets, and evidence IDs; they are the immediate inputs to hypothesis generation, not final claims by themselves.

Thread onlyThe literature clusters cleanly, but may not yet imply a new research question.
Gap + evidenceA plausible place to generate a hypothesis; check evidence IDs before trusting it.
TensionA stronger idea source when assumptions, benchmarks, or claims do not line up across papers.
Empty tension/connectionNot a failure by itself; it means the retrieved literature did not expose a clear contrast or transfer opportunity.

Map generation mode: llm. Fallback used: False. Reason: LLM returned a valid literature map with at least one thread and one gap.

Threads

  • T1 Linear representation vs. linear accessibility: P1 separates LRH into two claims: linear representation (features linearly embedded) and linear accessibility (features linearly decodable). P1 proves that linear accessibility requires quadratically more neurons (d = Ω(k^2 log k log(m/k))) than classical compressed sensing (d = O(k log(m/k))), establishing a quantitative gap.
    Evidence: Evidence 8 Evidence 12
  • T2 Formalizing linear representation via counterfactuals: P2 formalizes linear representation using counterfactuals in both output (word) and input (sentence) spaces, connecting to linear probing and model steering. It identifies a causal inner product that unifies geometric notions.
    Evidence: Evidence 16 Evidence 17

Gaps

  • G1: P1's theoretical bounds assume a single layer; it remains unknown how multiple layers can work together to store and access features, as noted in P1's discussion. Why it matters: Understanding multi-layer cooperation is crucial for explaining the full capability of deep language models.
    Evidence: Evidence 11
  • G2: P2's causal inner product is defined for a single layer; its extension to deeper layers and interaction with the compressed sensing bounds of P1 is unexplored. Why it matters: Bridging geometric formalization with capacity bounds could lead to a unified theory of representation in LLMs.
    Evidence: Evidence 17 Evidence 8

Tensions

  • X1: P1's lower bound shows linear decoding requires d = Ω(k^2 log k log(m/k)) neurons, while P2's counterfactual-based linear probing assumes linear decoding is feasible; the tension is whether practical LLMs meet this bound or rely on non-linear decoding.
    Evidence: Evidence 7 Evidence 16

Cross-Domain Connections

  • C1: P1 uses compressed sensing theory (signal processing) to bound feature storage in language models. The concept of linear compressed sensing can be transferred to other domains where linear decoding is required, such as neuroscience (neural population coding) or genomics (gene expression analysis). Rationale: The mathematical framework for linear compressed sensing applies to any system where features are linearly encoded and must be linearly decoded, such as neural populations representing stimuli.
    Evidence: Evidence 4 Evidence 5

5. Selected Hypothesis Portfolio

This section is the short answer: it presents the selected research-idea portfolio. By default the report keeps up to two hypotheses: a primary proposal and, when available, a distinct secondary or more incremental proposal. Each hypothesis is generated from the literature map and connected evidence, then scored for literature support, novelty, feasibility, cross-domain value, risk, obvious-extension penalty, and prior-work similarity. The primary hypothesis is used for novelty search and citation audit below. The secondary hypothesis is retained as a usable alternative, not as proof that it is equally novel or equally well supported.

Primary + high noveltyBest candidate for a new research direction, subject to novelty-search caveats.
Primary + high riskInteresting but fragile; prioritize feasibility and falsification before presenting it as a project.
Incremental secondaryUsable fallback or extension; expected to be less novel but often easier to test.
No evidence IDsIdea is mostly model inference; it should not be treated as source-supported.

Hypothesis generation mode: llm. Fallback used: False. Reason: LLM returned valid hypothesis candidates after binding and review.
Review: {'used': True, 'reason': 'LLM review returned replacement/refined hypotheses.', 'candidate_count': 2}

primary

H1 - In multi-layer language models, the effective number of features that can be linearly decoded from the final layer is greater than the bound for a single layer, because earlier layers can perform non-linear transformations that reduce feature sparsity or dimensionality before the final linear decoding layer.

Total score: 0.485 Inference: inferred Novelty: 0.7 Feasibility: 0.8 Obvious-extension penalty: 0.3

Why retained: Primary proposal selected for the main novelty search and citation audit. It is the strongest non-incremental candidate when available; otherwise it is the highest-scoring distinct candidate.

Motivation: P1's bounds assume a single layer with linear decoding. However, deep models have multiple layers that could transform features non-linearly, potentially circumventing the quadratic lower bound. P1's discussion (11) explicitly notes that multi-layer cooperation is not yet understood. This hypothesis explores whether the linear decoding bottleneck can be alleviated by earlier non-linear processing.

Novelty position: Extends P1's single-layer theory to multi-layer models, a gap explicitly acknowledged. If true, it would reconcile the theoretical lower bound with the empirical success of deep models.

How to test it: Design a two-layer network: first layer with non-linear activation (e.g., ReLU) that maps m features to a hidden representation, second layer linear. Vary d (neurons in second layer) and m, measure how many features can be linearly decoded from the second layer. Compare to P1's single-layer bound d = Ω(k^2 log k log(m/k)).

Premise evidence: Evidence 11 Evidence 7

Supporting papers: P1: How Many Features Can a Language Model Store Under the Linear Representation Hypothesis?

Risks:

  • The non-linear transformation might not reduce the effective k (sparsity) or might introduce new constraints that still lead to similar bounds.
  • Empirical validation on real LLMs may be confounded by other factors like training dynamics.
incremental

H2 - In practice, language models do not satisfy the linear decoding assumption for all features; instead, they rely on non-linear decoding for many features, which allows them to store exponentially many features per neuron as in the classical compressed sensing bound, consistent with the superposition hypothesis.

Total score: 0.34 Inference: inferred Novelty: 0.5 Feasibility: 0.9 Obvious-extension penalty: 0.5

Why retained: Retained as a more conservative A+B, transfer, or implementation-adjacent direction that is distinct from the primary proposal. It may be useful follow-up work, but it should not be read as the most novel hypothesis.

Motivation: P1 shows that linear decoding imposes a quadratic lower bound, but empirical evidence suggests LLMs store many features (superposition). This tension suggests that either LLMs meet the quadratic bound (unlikely given scale) or they use non-linear decoding. This hypothesis posits the latter, which would align with the classical compressed sensing bound and explain superposition.

Novelty position: Directly addresses the tension between theory and practice. If true, it would imply that linear probing is not the right tool for many features, and that non-linear probes or other methods are needed.

How to test it: Select a set of m binary features (e.g., syntactic or semantic properties) and extract activations from a layer of a pretrained LLM (e.g., LLaMA-2). Train linear probes and non-linear probes (2-layer MLP) to predict each feature. Measure the maximum m such that probes achieve >90% accuracy as a function of d. Compare to P1's bounds.

Premise evidence: Evidence 7 Evidence 4 Evidence 8

Supporting papers: P1: How Many Features Can a Language Model Store Under the Linear Representation Hypothesis?

Risks:

  • Non-linear probes may overfit or require more data; careful control needed.
  • The definition of 'feature' may be ambiguous; need to use a well-defined set of concepts.

6. Hypothesis Quality Audit

This section audits the generated idea itself, not just the references attached to it. The hypothesis is decomposed into components such as idea core, motivation, novelty position, mechanism, feasibility, experiment, and risks. Each component receives a small relevance-ranked evidence bundle when directly relevant evidence exists; otherwise it is labeled as model-inferred or needing review. This avoids padding the hypothesis with weak citations while still showing which parts are grounded in evidence IDs.

Source-stated + GREENThe component restates source text with provenance; it is evidence, not novelty.
Evidence-inspired + YELLOWThe component is motivated by evidence but goes beyond what papers directly prove.
Model-inferred + YELLOWThe component is a generated bridge or mechanism; it needs human review and experiments.
Unsupported / REDThe component lacks evidence, contradicts evidence, or makes an unsupported strong claim.

Summary: Hypothesis quality audit separates what the literature directly says from what the model inferred as a new idea. Generated idea, novelty, and experiment components should usually be YELLOW unless directly restating an established finding.

Components: 16; by status: {'evidence_inspired': 10, 'model_inferred': 1, 'needs_human_review': 5}; by color: {'yellow': 16}; precision evidence per component: None

While the present work has established how a single layer can linearly represent many features in a way that a subsequent layer can easily retrieve, there remains much to be understood in terms of how this capability can enable multiple layers to work in tandem to achieve the remarkable phenomena we have observed. Overall, our work lays out mathematical foundations for the linear representation hypothesis as a basis for the theoretical study of language models.

literature map X1: context
Informally, the “linear representation hypothesis” is the idea that high-level concepts are represented linearly as directions in some representation space. To answer these, we use the language of counterfactuals to give two formalizations of linear representation, one in the output (word) representation space, and one in the input (context) space. We then prove that these connect to linear probing and model steering, respectively.
Evidence 17literature map T2, literature map G2The Linear Representation Hypothesis and the Geometry of Large Language Modelspdf:abstract:p1
literature map T2: context
literature map G2: context
We then prove that these connect to linear probing and model steering, respectively. To make sense of geometric notions, we use the formalization to identify a particular (non-Euclidean) inner product that respects language structure in a sense we make precise. Using this causal inner product, we show how to unify all notions of linear representation.

We introduce a mathematical framework for the linear representation hypothesis (LRH), which asserts that intermediate layers of language models store features linearly. We separate the hypothesis into two claims: linear representation (features are linearly embedded in neuron activations) and linear accessibility (features can be linearly decoded). Classical results in compressed sensing imply that for k-sparse inputs, d = O(k log(m/k)) suffices if we allow non-linear decoding algorithms (Candes and Tao, 2006; Cand`es et al., 2006; Donoho, 2006).

We separate the hypothesis into two claims: linear representation (features are linearly embedded in neuron activations) and linear accessibility (features can be linearly decoded). Classical results in compressed sensing imply that for k-sparse inputs, d = O(k log(m/k)) suffices if we allow non-linear decoding algorithms (Candes and Tao, 2006; Cand`es et al., 2006; Donoho, 2006). However, the additional requirement of linear decoding takes the problem out of the classical compressed sensing, into linear compressed sensing.
Evidence 23CL1 supportHow Many Features Can a Language Model Store Under the Linear Representation Hypothesis?pdf:abstract:p1
CL1 support: indirect
Uncertain or partial support: Available evidence motivates this generated claim, but it cannot directly prove the full claim.
Classical results in compressed sensing imply that for k-sparse inputs, d = O(k log(m/k)) suffices if we allow non-linear decoding algorithms (Candes and Tao, 2006; Cand`es et al., 2006; Donoho, 2006). However, the additional requirement of linear decoding takes the problem out of the classical compressed sensing, into linear compressed sensing. We prove that d = Ωϵ( k2 log k log(m/k)) is required while d = Oϵ(k2 log m) suffices. The lower bound establishes a quantitative gap between classical and linear compressed setting, illustrating how linear accessibility is a meaningfully stronger hypothesis than linear representation alone. The upper bound confirms that neurons can store an exponential number of features under the LRH, giving theoretical evidence for the “superposition hypothesis” (Elhage et al., 2022).
Evidence 24CL1 support, CL2 supportHow Many Features Can a Language Model Store Under the Linear Representation Hypothesis?pdf:abstract:p1
CL1 support: indirect
Uncertain or partial support: Available evidence motivates this generated claim, but it cannot directly prove the full claim.
CL2 support: indirect
Uncertain or partial support: Available evidence motivates this generated claim, but it cannot directly prove the full claim.
The lower bound proof uses rank bounds for near-identity matrices (Alon, 2003) together with Tur´an’s theorem (bounding the number of edges in clique-free graphs). We also show how our results do and do not constrain the geometry of feature representations and extend our results to allow decoders with an activation function and bias.

7. Hypothesis Novelty Search

This section searches for close prior work after the selected hypothesis is generated. After the primary hypothesis is selected, the system creates novelty-search queries from the hypothesis text, method terms, target problem, and supporting-paper neighborhood, then searches external literature again. The result is a risk assessment: exact/close matches suggest the idea may already exist, near matches suggest the idea needs sharper differentiation, related works provide background, and missing results only mean no close prior work was found by available tools. This section never proves novelty conclusively.

High riskExact or close prior work found; the idea likely needs substantial differentiation.
Medium riskNear work shares method, target problem, or framing; novelty may survive only with a sharper mechanism.
Low riskOnly background-related work found; still not proof of novelty.
Unknown riskSearch failed or evidence is too sparse; do not claim novelty.

Novelty risk: LOW

Conclusion: No close prior work was found by available tools, but related background work exists.

Reason: 9 related work(s) were found; absence of a close match is not proof of novelty.

8. Cited Reference Integrity Audit

This section audits only the references actually cited by the selected hypothesis atomic claims. It answers a bibliographic question: does the cited reference appear to exist, and do title, authors, year, DOI, venue, or source URL point to the same paper? It deliberately does not judge whether the reference supports the scientific claim; that is handled in the next section. Field status values separate matched/present metadata from missing or uncertain metadata. Missing DOI/year usually means the field was unavailable or not comparable, not automatically that the reference is wrong.

Score is bibliographic identity confidence, not a metadata-completeness percentage. A searched bibliographic record or local input PDF can score highly even when DOI or year is missing, but non-seed references with both DOI and year missing are downgraded to YELLOW.

GREEN integrityReference identity is verified enough for audit use; claim support is still judged separately.
YELLOW integrityReference probably exists but metadata is incomplete or uncertain; support cannot be upgraded to strong confidence.
RED integrityReference is not found or metadata conflicts strongly; related claim-support rows should be RED.
Missing DOI/yearUsually source coverage limitation, not a contradiction, unless other metadata also conflicts.

9. Claim Support Audit

This section audits whether each cited reference supports the specific atomic claim attached to it. Rows in this table are claim-reference pairs, so the same claim ID may appear more than once if multiple references are checked separately. This is stricter than bibliographic integrity: a real paper can be GREEN in the previous section but YELLOW or RED here if it only provides background, supports a weaker claim, discusses a different topic, or lacks usable evidence snippets.

Support type rule: direct means verified reference + explicit evidence for the specific claim; indirect means topic-relevant evidence that motivates but does not prove the claim; background means weak/abstract-only context; absent means no usable evidence or no valid reference.

Error category rule: no_specific_error_detected means this row was not classified as unsupported claim, overclaim, mis-citation, or contradiction. It does not mean the claim is proven; use Color, Support, Support Type, Evidence IDs, and Reason for the final interpretation.

10. Evidence Cards

Evidence Cards expand evidence IDs into the human-readable form used throughout the report. Each card shows the source paper, URL or path, location, snippet text, and every claim or map item that reused the evidence. The same snippet can support multiple claims in different ways, so each use has its own support type, error category, and reason. Internal hash IDs are kept only in Appendix B for reproducibility; the numbered Evidence labels are intended for review.

One evidence, one useSimple case: one snippet is used for one claim or map item.
One evidence, multiple usesEach use has its own support type and reason; do not infer one global label for the snippet.
Abstract locationUseful but shallow; direct support from abstract alone should be interpreted cautiously.
PDF/full-text locationUsually stronger provenance because the snippet comes from a section or page-level source.

While the present work has established how a single layer can linearly represent many features in a way that a subsequent layer can easily retrieve, there remains much to be understood in terms of how this capability can enable multiple layers to work in tandem to achieve the remarkable phenomena we have observed. We prove that d = Ωϵ( k2 log k log(m/k)) is required while d = Oϵ(k2 log m) suffices. Informally, the “linear representation hypothesis” is the idea that high-level concepts are represented linearly as directions in some representation space. To answer these, we use the language of counterfactuals to give two formalizations of linear representation, one in the output (word) representation space, and one in the input (context) space. We then prove that these connect to linear probing and model steering, respectively.

We then prove that these connect to linear probing and model steering, respectively. To make sense of geometric notions, we use the formalization to identify a particular (non-Euclidean) inner product that respects language structure in a sense we make precise. Using this causal inner product, we show how to unify all notions of linear representation.

We introduce a mathematical framework for the linear representation hypothesis (LRH), which asserts that intermediate layers of language models store features linearly. We separate the hypothesis into two claims: linear representation (features are linearly embedded in neuron activations) and linear accessibility (features can be linearly decoded). Classical results in compressed sensing imply that for k-sparse inputs, d = O(k log(m/k)) suffices if we allow non-linear decoding algorithms (Candes and Tao, 2006; Cand`es et al., 2006; Donoho, 2006).

Classical results in compressed sensing imply that for k-sparse inputs, d = O(k log(m/k)) suffices if we allow non-linear decoding algorithms (Candes and Tao, 2006; Cand`es et al., 2006; Donoho, 2006). 11. Controlled Citation Audit Cases

This section is a controlled capability demonstration, separate from the selected hypothesis. The system constructs one direct-support case, one uncertain or inferred case, and one deliberately wrong or nonexistent-reference case. These probes show whether the audit machinery can produce GREEN, YELLOW, and RED under known conditions. They should not be interpreted as extra scientific claims made by the hypothesis generator.

GREEN controlled caseKnown direct support case; should have evidence and pass as GREEN.
YELLOW controlled caseGenerated or inferred claim; should not be forced to GREEN.
RED controlled caseFake reference, contradiction, or unsupported absolute claim; should never become GREEN.
Failing controlled caseIndicates an audit rule regression rather than a scientific finding.

14. Memory Influence Summary

This section explains the role of persistent SQLite memory in the current run. Memory can store prior papers, references, hypotheses, evidence records, rejected evidence, and verification outcomes. Retrieval does not automatically become evidence: previous memory may influence query planning, flag duplicate-risk, or warn about previously rejected material, but direct verification is reused only when the current claim, reference, evidence, and rule version match. If memory was retrieved but did not materially affect the run, the report says so explicitly.

Retrieved + materially alteredMemory changed query planning, duplicate-risk assessment, or direct verification reuse.
Retrieved + not usedMemory was available but treated as context only; this is acceptable and should be reported honestly.
Duplicate risk highA similar previous hypothesis exists; novelty should be interpreted cautiously.
Negative memoryPreviously rejected snippets or noisy candidates are warning signals, never scientific counter-evidence by themselves.

Memory influenced query planning/context through same-scope paper hits or memory-derived queries.

Negative Memory

These are previous rejected evidence snippets or failed candidates. They are warning-only memories: useful for avoiding repeated retrieval mistakes, identifying noisy sources, or explaining why a candidate was ignored, but never evidence that a scientific claim is false. Scientific support and contradiction must still come from current, inspectable evidence snippets.

  • Novelty search risk is low; no confirmed novelty is implied.
  • Evidence table contains 20 record(s).
  • Reference integrity audit produced 1 row(s): {'green': 1}.
  • Claim support audit produced 3 row(s): {'yellow': 3}.
  • Controlled audit cases demonstrate GREEN, YELLOW, and RED behavior.
  • Memory influence summary is present.
  • Reference cleaning summary recorded 3 clean references and 0 unresolved fragment(s).

Recommended Next Actions

  • Keep generated hypothesis claims YELLOW unless direct evidence cards prove an already-established subclaim.

16. Warnings and Limitations

This section collects automatic warnings and limitations detected during the run. Warnings are not automatic failures, but they mark places where a human reviewer should be cautious: shallow evidence, few retrieved papers, all-YELLOW claim support, high novelty risk, weak memory influence, API fallback, noisy reference extraction, or generated claims that remain mostly inferred.

No warningsThe automated checks found no obvious limitation, but human review is still required.
Evidence warningsSupport may be shallow, abstract-only, or missing full-text provenance.
Retrieval warningsFew connected papers, API fallback, or noisy search means the map may be incomplete.
Hypothesis warningsThe final idea may be mostly inferred, low-scoring, or duplicate-risk.
  • All selected-hypothesis claim support results are YELLOW; the generated idea is mostly inferred and needs new experiments.
  • Some hypothesis components are model-inferred without direct evidence IDs; review the Hypothesis Quality Audit before treating them as conclusions.
  • Some selected hypotheses are grounded only in input-paper evidence (H2); treat them as within-paper extrapolations unless external supporting papers are added.
  • Generated hypothesis support is mainly indirect, background, or absent.

Real API mode depends on public endpoints, available abstracts/full text, and external service coverage. Absence of retrieved close prior work is not proof of novelty.

Appendix A. Search and Retrieval Details

This appendix expands the retrieval pipeline. It shows the planned pre-generation queries, optional semantic-neighborhood expansion, kept papers, rejected papers, and the relatedness/citation-chain audit used to justify filtering. Kept papers are allowed into literature mapping and hypothesis generation; rejected papers are preserved for transparency but should not be treated as supporting evidence.

Kept paperPassed relatedness/citation-chain gate and can enter mapping or hypothesis generation.
Rejected paperRecorded for transparency but excluded from support and hypothesis generation.
Expansion enabledGraph neighborhood was sparse, so the system searched semantically nearby papers.
Expansion disabledConnected pool was already sufficient or run mode did not require expansion.

Semantic Neighborhood Expansion

Enabled: True. Citation/reference graph produced a sparse candidate pool, so the controller ran a domain-agnostic title/abstract semantic-neighborhood expansion.

Query generation: llm_with_deterministic_fallback

Policy: When an LLM is enabled, expansion queries are generated from input-paper and connected-paper titles/abstracts; deterministic title/abstract keyword queries are kept as fallback. They do not inject domain-specific answers. Added candidates are marked as semantic_expansion_candidate and must still pass relatedness scoring; they are not treated as citation evidence.

Connected papers before expansion: 3; trigger threshold: 8; candidates added: 24.

9(E-c2256fb24e03)full_text_pdfinput_pdfHow Many Features Can a Language Model Store Under the Linear Representation Hypothesis?pdf:abstract:p10.95We introduce a mathematical framework for the linear representation hypothesis (LRH), which asserts that intermediate layers of language models store features linearly. We separate the hypothesis into two claims: linear representation (features are linearly embedded in neuron activations) and linear accessibility (features can be linearly decoded). Classical results in compressed sensing imply that for k-sparse inputs, d = O(k log(m/k)) suffices if we allow non-linear decoding algorithms (Candes and Tao, 2006; Cand`es et al., 2006; Donoho, 2006).
10(E-41cb382455e9)full_text_pdfinput_pdfHow Many Features Can a Language Model Store Under the Linear Representation Hypothesis?pdf:introduction:p10.95Towards understanding how language models work, a single principle has been remarkably useful: the Linear Representation Hypothesis (LRH) states that intermediate layers of language models store features linearly. Alain and Bengio (2016) demonstrated empirically that in deeper layers of vision models, features (e.g., the presence of cats or dogs) could increasingly be extracted using linear classifiers trained on activations, suggesting that deep neural networks work to arrange features linearly. Since then, work has shown that a wide range of features can be accessed using these “linear probes,” such as sentiment and game state (Nanda et al., 2023; Li et al., 2025).
11(E-ebe4c4e5c9a4)full_text_pdfinput_pdfHow Many Features Can a Language Model Store Under the Linear Representation Hypothesis?pdf:discussion:p180.95While the present work has established how a single layer can linearly represent many features in a way that a subsequent layer can easily retrieve, there remains much to be understood in terms of how this capability can enable multiple layers to work in tandem to achieve the remarkable phenomena we have observed. Overall, our work lays out mathematical foundations for the linear representation hypothesis as a basis for the theoretical study of language models.
12(E-93c1fefcfaa6)full_text_pdfinput_pdfHow Many Features Can a Language Model Store Under the Linear Representation Hypothesis?pdf:abstract:p10.95We prove that d = Ωϵ( k2 log k log(m/k)) is required while d = Oϵ(k2 log m) suffices. The lower bound establishes a quantitative gap between classical and linear compressed setting, illustrating how linear accessibility is a meaningfully stronger hypothesis than linear representation alone. The upper bound confirms that neurons can store an exponential number of features under the LRH, giving theoretical evidence for the “superposition hypothesis” (Elhage et al., 2022).
13(E-f694992da50e)full_text_pdfinput_pdfHow Many Features Can a Language Model Store Under the Linear Representation Hypothesis?pdf:abstract:p10.95The lower bound establishes a quantitative gap between classical and linear compressed setting, illustrating how linear accessibility is a meaningfully stronger hypothesis than linear representation alone. The upper bound confirms that neurons can store an exponential number of features under the LRH, giving theoretical evidence for the “superposition hypothesis” (Elhage et al., 2022). The upper bound proof uses standard random constructions of matrices with approximately orthogonal columns.
14(E-417f960dbc49)full_text_pdfinput_pdfHow Many Features Can a Language Model Store Under the Linear Representation Hypothesis?pdf:results:p20.95In the present work, we aim to give a mathematical framework capable of addressing the following fundamental question (stated informally here): (*) Under the linear representation hypothesis, how many features can one layer of d neurons store? Then how many features can this vector store, and under what conditions? The present work illustrates one reason why there is a lack of clarity surrounding this basic question: different formulations of the LRH imply different answers to the question.
15(E-571fa8808a0e)full_text_pdfopenalexThe Linear Representation Hypothesis and the Geometry of Large Language Modelspdf:introduction:p10.95In the context of language models, the “Linear Representation Hypothesis” is the idea that high-level concepts are represented linearly in the representation space of a model (e.g., Mikolov et al., 2013c; Arora et al., 2016; Elhage et al., 2022). High-level concepts might include: is the text in French or English?
16(E-cc435943b924)full_text_pdfopenalexThe Linear Representation Hypothesis and the Geometry of Large Language Modelspdf:abstract:p10.95Informally, the “linear representation hypothesis” is the idea that high-level concepts are represented linearly as directions in some representation space. To answer these, we use the language of counterfactuals to give two formalizations of linear representation, one in the output (word) representation space, and one in the input (context) space. We then prove that these connect to linear probing and model steering, respectively.
17(E-092546eab711)full_text_pdfopenalexThe Linear Representation Hypothesis and the Geometry of Large Language Modelspdf:abstract:p10.95We then prove that these connect to linear probing and model steering, respectively. To make sense of geometric notions, we use the formalization to identify a particular (non-Euclidean) inner product that respects language structure in a sense we make precise. Using this causal inner product, we show how to unify all notions of linear representation.

20(E-838c6fed3385)full_text_pdfopenalexThe Linear Representation Hypothesis and the Geometry of Large Language Modelspdf:background:p30.95We use a cone instead of subspace because the sign of the difference is significant—i.e., the difference between “king” and “queen” is in the opposite direction as the difference between “woman” and “man”. The unembedding representation (if it exists) is unique up to positive scaling, consistent with the linear subspace hypothesis that concepts are represented as directions. Connection to Measurement The first result is that the unembedding representation is closely tied to the measurement notion of linear representation: Theorem 2.2 (Measurement Representation). In words: if we know the output token is either “king” or “queen” (say, the context was about a monarch), then the probability that the output is “king” is logit-linear in the language model representation with regression coefficients ¯γW . The random scalar α is a function of the particular counterfactual pair {Y (0), Y (1)}—e.g., it may be different for {“king”, “queen”} and {“roi”, “reine”}.

Evidence Snippets with Provenance

CL1 - YELLOW

We introduce a mathematical framework for the linear representation hypothesis (LRH), which asserts that intermediate layers of language models store features linearly. We separate the hypothesis into two claims: linear representation (features are linearly embedded in neuron activations) and linear accessibility (features can be linearly decoded). Classical results in compressed sensing imply that for k-sparse inputs, d = O(k log(m/k)) suffices if we allow non-linear decoding algorithms (Candes and Tao, 2006; Cand`es et al., 2006; Donoho, 2006).
Evidence ID: 21 (E-124f0fb52601); Source: input_pdf; Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Paper: How Many Features Can a Language Model Store Under the Linear Representation Hypothesis?; URL: D:\SciHypoCheck\reports\openclaw_inputs\openclaw_input_20260605_011309.pdf; Confidence: 0.95
We separate the hypothesis into two claims: linear representation (features are linearly embedded in neuron activations) and linear accessibility (features can be linearly decoded). Classical results in compressed sensing imply that for k-sparse inputs, d = O(k log(m/k)) suffices if we allow non-linear decoding algorithms (Candes and Tao, 2006; Cand`es et al., 2006; Donoho, 2006). However, the additional requirement of linear decoding takes the problem out of the classical compressed sensing, into linear compressed sensing.
Evidence ID: 22 (E-afe80db72910); Source: input_pdf; Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Paper: How Many Features Can a Language Model Store Under the Linear Representation Hypothesis?; URL: D:\SciHypoCheck\reports\openclaw_inputs\openclaw_input_20260605_011309.pdf; Confidence: 0.95
Classical results in compressed sensing imply that for k-sparse inputs, d = O(k log(m/k)) suffices if we allow non-linear decoding algorithms (Candes and Tao, 2006; Cand`es et al., 2006; Donoho, 2006). However, the additional requirement of linear decoding takes the problem out of the classical compressed sensing, into linear compressed sensing. We prove that d = Ωϵ( k2 log k log(m/k)) is required while d = Oϵ(k2 log m) suffices. The lower bound establishes a quantitative gap between classical and linear compressed setting, illustrating how linear accessibility is a meaningfully stronger hypothesis than linear representation alone. The upper bound confirms that neurons can store an exponential number of features under the LRH, giving theoretical evidence for the “superposition hypothesis” (Elhage et al., 2022).
Evidence ID: 23 (E-b620aa8304a6); Source: input_pdf; Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Paper: How Many Features Can a Language Model Store Under the Linear Representation Hypothesis?; URL: D:\SciHypoCheck\reports\openclaw_inputs\openclaw_input_20260605_011309.pdf; Confidence: 0.95
The lower bound proof uses rank bounds for near-identity matrices (Alon, 2003) together with Tur´an’s theorem (bounding the number of edges in clique-free graphs). We also show how our results do and do not constrain the geometry of feature representations and extend our results to allow decoders with an activation function and bias.
Evidence ID: 24 (E-952c3cbb5f35); Source: input_pdf; Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Paper: How Many Features Can a Language Model Store Under the Linear Representation Hypothesis?; URL: D:\SciHypoCheck\reports\openclaw_inputs\openclaw_input_20260605_011309.pdf; Confidence: 0.95
The lower bound establishes a quantitative gap between classical and linear compressed setting, illustrating how linear accessibility is a meaningfully stronger hypothesis than linear representation alone. The upper bound confirms that neurons can store an exponential number of features under the LRH, giving theoretical evidence for the “superposition hypothesis” (Elhage et al., 2022). The upper bound proof uses standard random constructions of matrices with approximately orthogonal columns.

CL3 - YELLOW

We introduce a mathematical framework for the linear representation hypothesis (LRH), which asserts that intermediate layers of language models store features linearly. We separate the hypothesis into two claims: linear representation (features are linearly embedded in neuron activations) and linear accessibility (features can be linearly decoded). Classical results in compressed sensing imply that for k-sparse inputs, d = O(k log(m/k)) suffices if we allow non-linear decoding algorithms (Candes and Tao, 2006; Cand`es et al., 2006; Donoho, 2006).

We separate the hypothesis into two claims: linear representation (features are linearly embedded in neuron activations) and linear accessibility (features can be linearly decoded). Evidence ID: 22 (E-afe80db72910); Source: input_pdf; Location: pdf:abstract:p1; Paper: How Many Features Can a Language Model Store Under the Linear Representation Hypothesis?; URL: D:\SciHypoCheck\reports\openclaw_inputs\openclaw_input_20260605_011309.pdf; Confidence: 0.95
We prove that d = Ωϵ( k2 log k log(m/k)) is required while d = Oϵ(k2 log m) suffices. The lower bound establishes a quantitative gap between classical and linear compressed setting, illustrating how linear accessibility is a meaningfully stronger hypothesis than linear representation alone. Atomic Claims

  • CL1: P1's bounds assume a single layer with linear decoding. However, deep models have multiple layers that could transform features non-linearly, potentially circumventing the quadratic lower bound. P1's discussion (11) explicitly notes that multi-layer cooperation is not yet understood. This hypothesis explores whether the linear decoding bottleneck can be alleviated by earlier non-linear processing. (gap_motivation)
    Evidence IDs: 11(E-ebe4c4e5c9a4) 7(E-35adeea3493c)
  • CL2: Extends P1's single-layer theory to multi-layer models, a gap explicitly acknowledged. If true, it would reconcile the theoretical lower bound with the empirical success of deep models. (novelty_rationale)
    Evidence IDs: 11(E-ebe4c4e5c9a4) 7(E-35adeea3493c)
  • CL3: In multi-layer language models, the effective number of features that can be linearly decoded from the final layer is greater than the bound for a single layer, because earlier layers can perform non-linear transformations that reduce feature sparsity or dimensionality before the final linear decoding layer. (novel_claim_requires_experiment)
    Evidence IDs: 11(E-ebe4c4e5c9a4) 7(E-35adeea3493c)
